1. 
INEQUALITIES 
1.1. Introduction. 
Throughout this chapter we are concerned with real numbers only. 
When two real numbers are not equal, a relation of inequality is said 
to exist between them. The property of trichotomy in the set of all real 
numbers states that any two real numbers a, b must satisfy one and only 
one of the following relations-
(i) 
a is equal to b 
(a= b), 
(ii) 
a is greater than b (a> b), 
(iii) 
a is less than b 
(a< b). 
The last two relations are inequality relations. 
Therefore, if a be a real number different from 0, one of the following 
inequality relations must hold-
(i) a > 0, 
(ii) a < 0. 
When a > 0, a is said to be positive; when a < 0, a is said to be 
negative. 
We define a > b if a - b > 0, and 
a < b if a - b < 0. 
The relations a > b and b < a state the same inequality relation, since 
a> 0 ~-a< 0. 
The symbol a > 
b means a is greater than or equal to b; 
a < b means a is less than or equal to b. 
For example, if a is a real number then a2 > O; 
if a is a positive real number then a2 > 0. 
1.2. Properties. 
If a, b, c be real numbers, then 
(i) 
a > b and b > e =? a > e, 
(ii) 
a > b =? a+ e > b + e, 
(iii) 
a> band e > 0 =? ae > be, 
(iv) 
a > b and e < 0 =? ae < be, 
( v) 
a > b and e = 0 =? ae = be.

Theorem 1.2.1. If ai, a2, . . . , an; bi, b2, ... , bn be all real numbers such 
that ai > bi for i = 1, 2, ... , n, then 
ai + a2 + · · · + an > bi + b2 + · · · + bn. 
Proof. (ai + a2 +· · ·+an) - (bi + b2 + · · · + bn) 
= ( ai - bi) + ( a2 - b2) + · · · + ( an - bn) 
> 
0, since ai - bi> 0 for i = 1,2, ... ,n. 
Therefore ai + a2 +···+an > bi + b2 + · · · + bn. 
Theorem 1.2.2. If ai, a2, ... , an; bi, b2, ... , bn be all positive real mun-
hers such that ai > bi for i = 1, 2, ... , n, then 
aia2 .. . an > bib2 ... bn. 
Proof. aia2 - bib2 = ai (a2 - b2) + b2(ai - bi) 
> 0, since each term is positive. 
Therefore aia2 > bib2. 
Thus ai > bi and a2 > b2 => aia2 > bib2. 
Similarly, aia2 > b1b2 and a3 > b3 => a1a2a3 > b1b2b3• 
Successive applications give a1 a2 .. . an > b1 b2 ... bn. 
Note. The theorem does not hold if the numbers be not all positive. 
For example, 5 > -2 and 2 > -10 but 5.2 < (-2).(-10).

Theorem 1.2.3. If a, b be positive real numbers with a > b, and n be a 
positive integer, then an > bn. 
Proof. The theorem holds for n = 1. 
Let us assume that the theorem holds for a positive integer, say m. 
Then am> bm. 
Now am> bm > 0 and a> b > 0:::::} am+l > bm+1. 
This shows that the theorem holds for n = m + l if it holds for n = m. 
And the theorem holds for n = I. 
By the principle of induction, the theorem holds for all positive inte-
gers n. 
Note. If a, b are real numbers with a > band n is a positive integer, it 
does not necessarily follow that an > bn. 
For example, 2 > -3 implies (2) 2 < (-3)2 , but 2 > -1 implies 
(2)2 > (-1 )2.

4. If a, b, c be all positive real numbers and n be a positive rational 
number, prove that 
an(a - b)(a - c) + bn(b - a)(b - c) + cn(c - a)(c - b) > 0. 
Case 1. Let a = b = c. 
L.H.S.= 0, since each term is 0. 
Case 2. Let two of a, b, c be equal. a= b, say. 
L.H.S.= cn(c - a)2 > 0, since c > 0, (c - a) 2 > 0. 
Case 3. No two of a, b, c are equal. 
Without loss of generality, let a > b > c. 
Then a - b > 0, b - c > 0, a - c > 0. 
an(a - b)(a - c) + bn(b - a)(b - c) = (a - b)[an(a - c) - bn(b - c)]. 
a > b > c =} a - -~
b - c > 0 and a > b > 0 =} an > bn, since n > 0. 
Therefore an(a - c) > bn(b - c), by Theorem 1.2.2. 
Hence an(a - b)(a - c) + bn(b - a)(b - c) > 0. 
Also we have cn(c - a)(c - b) > 0. 
Therefore an(a - b)(a - c) + bn(b - a)(b - c) + cn(c - a)(c - b) > 0. 
Combining all cases, we have 
an(a - b)(a - c) + bn(b- a)(b- c) + cn(c - a)(c - b) > 0, the equality 
occurs when a= b = c.

The ordered array of n real numbers a1 , a2, ... , an is denoted by 
(a1, a2, .. . , an). Let (a) = (a1, a2, ... , an), (b) = (b1, b2, ... , bn). Then 
(a) = (b) if ai = bi for i = 1, 2, .. . , n. The ordered arrays (a) and (b) are 
said to be proportional if there exists a non-zero real number k such that 
ai = kbi for i = 1,2, ... ,n. 
For example, (1, 1, 2, 0, -1) and (2, 2, 4, 0, -2) are proportional. 
2. Cauchy-Schwarz inequality. 
If a1 , a2, ... , an; b1, b2, ... , bn be all real numbers, then 
( a 1 2 + a2 2 + · · · + an 2) ( b1 2 + b2 2 + · · · + bn 2) > ( a 1 b1 + a2 b2 + · · · + an bn) 2, 
the equality occurs when 
either (i) 
ai = 0 for i = 1, 2, ... , n; or bi = 0 for i = 1, 2, ... , n; or both 
ai = 0 and bi = 0 for i = 1, 2, . . . , n; 
or (ii) 
ai = kbi for some non-zero real k, i = 1, 2, ... , n.

This completes the proof.

2. COMPLEX NUMBERS 
2.1. Introduction. 
It follows from the properties of real numbers that the square of a 
real number is never negative. Consequently, the elementary quadratic 
equation x2 + 1 = 0 has no solution in the system of real numbers. 
Introduction of a new type of numbers, called complex numbers, has 
made it possible to provide solutions of the equation x2 + 1 = 0 and also 
of the more general type of equations 
aoxn + a1xn-l +·· · +an= 0, 
where ao, a1, ... , an are real numbers. 
2.2. Complex numbers. 
Definition. A complex number z is defined to be an ordered pair of real 
numbers (a, b) that satisfies the following condition (i) and the following 
laws of operations (ii) and (iii) 
(i) 
(a, b) = (c, d) if and only if a= c, b = d (condition of equality) 
(ii) 
(a, b) + (c, d) = (a+ c, b + d) (definition of addition) 
(iii) 
(a, b).(c, d) = (ac - bd, ad+ be) (definition of multiplication). 
Of the ordered pair ( a, b), the first component a is said to be the real 
part of z and is denoted by Rl z and the second component b is said to 
be the imaginary part of z and is denoted by Im z. 
Theorem 2.2.1. Addition of complex numbers is commutative. 
This says that if z1, z2 be complex numbers then z1 + z2 = z2 + z1. 
Proof. Let zi = (a1, b1), z2 = (a2, b2), 
Then z1 + z2 = (a1 + a2, b1 + b2), z2 + z1 = (a2 + a1, b2 + bi). 
But a 1 + a2 = a2 + a 1 , b1 + b2 = b2 +bi, since addition of real numbers 
Is commutative. 
Therefore zi + z2 = z2 + z1.

Theorem 2.2.3. Multiplication of complex numbers is commutative.

The complex numbers of the form (a, 0) having imaginary part Oare 
of special interest. For such complex numbers 
(a1, 0) + (a2, 0) = (a1 + a2, 0) and (a1, 0).(a2, 0) = (a1a2, 0). 
It is evident that the operations of addition and multiplication for 
such complex numbers take the form of arithmetic addition and multi-
plication for real numbers. 
The complex number (a, 0) is identified with the real number a. vVe 
write a= (a, 0). In particular, 0 = (0, 0). 
The complex numbers of the form (0, b) having the real part 0 and the 
imaginary part non-zero are of special interest. Such a complex number 
is said to be an imaginary number. For such complex numbers 
(0, b1) + (0, b2) = (0, b1 + b2) and 
(0, b1).(0, b2) = (-b1b2, 0). 
The sum of two imaginary numbers is imaginary and the product of 
two such numbers is real. 
In particular, the complex number (0, 1) is said to be the imaginary 
unit and it is denoted by i. 
Thus i.i = (0, 1).(0, 1) = (-1, 0) = -1. 
Also (0, b) = (0, l).(b, 0) = (b, 0).(0, l). 
Hence (0, b) can be expressed as ib or bi.

2.4. Geometrical representation. 
Just as real numbers are represented as points on a line, complex 
numbers (which are also ordered pairs of real numbers) can be represented 
as points on a plane. 
With respect to a given rectangular co-ordinate system in a plane, 
the complex number z = a + bi can be represented by the point with 
co-ordinates ( a, b). 
The first co-ordinate axis is called the real axis and the second co-
ordinate axis is called the imaginary axis. 
The plane in which this representation is made is said to be the com-
plex plane, or the Gaussian plane, after the name of Gauss, a celebrated 
German mathematician. 
The origin represents the zero complex number. The points on the 
real axis represent all real numbers of the form ( a, 0) and the points on the 
imaginary axis, other than the origin, represent all imaginary numbers 
of the form (0, b). 
2.5. Conjugate of a complex number. 
Let z =a+ bi be a complex number. The conjugate of z, denoted by 
z, is defined to be the complex number a - bi. 
Thus z == a - bi when z = a + bi. 
Geometrically, the point z is the reflection of the point z in the real axis

2. 7. Polar form. 
Let z = a+ bi be a complex number. In the complex plane z is 
represented by the point whose Cartesian co-ordinates are ( a, b) referred 
to two perpendicular lines as axes, the first co-ordinate axis being called 
the real axis and the second the imaginary axis. 
Taking the origin as the pole and the real axis as the initial line, let 
( r, 0) be the polar co-ordinates of the point ( a, b). Then a = r cos 0, b = 
r sin 0. 
Geometrically, r is the distance of the point ( a, b) from the origin. r 
is said to be the modulus of the complex number z. 
For every point other than the origin, r > 0. For the point (0, 0), 
r = 0. Thus every non-zero complex number has a positive modulus. 
0 is the angle made by the radius vector through the point ( a, b) with 
the real axis. 0 is called an argument or an amplitude of z. 
0 cannot be determined for the zero complex number. 
Consequently, a non-zero complex number z is represented in the form 
z = r ( cos 0 + i sin 0). 
This is called the polar form or modulus-amplitude form of the com-
plex number z. 
As 0 is indeterminate for the zero complex number, the zero complex 
number has no polar representation. 
For a non-zero complex number z, 0 has infinitely many values dif-
fering from one another by a multiple of 21r. 
If z = r( cos 0 + i sin 0), r is determined from the relations r cos 0 = 
a, r sin 0 = b giving r = ✓a2 + b2 and 0 is determined from the relations 
cos 0 = £, sin 0 = Q. 
r 
r 
All values of 0 are expressed as Arg z ( or Amp z). If a be a value of () 
satisfying the relations cos 0 =£, sin0 =Q then Arg z (Amp z) = a+2n1r, 
r 
r 
n being an integer. 
The principal argument (amplitude) of z, denoted by arg z (amp z), 
is defined to be the angle 0 which satisfies the inequality -1r < 0 < 1r. 
For example, 3; is an argument of the complex number -i but it is 
not the principal argument because 0( = 3;) does not satisfy the relation 
~1r < 0 < 1r. The principal argument of -i is -; and Arg (-i) = 
T + 2n1r, or - i + 2m1r, where n and m are integers. 
Note. An argument of the complex nu1nber a+ bi is to be determined 
from the relations cos 0 = 53:, sin 0 = !!. simultaneously and not from the

Theorem 2.8.1. De Moivre's theorem 
When n is an integer, positive or negative, and 0 is a real number 
( COS 0 + i sin 0)n =COS n0 + i sin n0; 
when n is a fraction, positive or negative, and 0 is a real number 
cos n0 + i sin n0 is one of the values of ( cos 0 + i sin or. 
Proof Case I. Let n be a positive integer. 
The theorem holds for n = l, since (cos +i sin 0) 1 = cos B+i sin 0 = 
cos 10 + i sin 10. 
Let us assume that the theorem holds for n = m , where m is a positive 
integer. 
Then (cos 0 + i sin 0)m =cos m0 + i sin m0. 
Therefore (cos 0 + i sin 0)m+l = (cos m0 + i sin m0)(cos 0 + i sin 0) 
= (cos m0cos 0-sin m0sin 0) + i(cos m0sin 0+sin m0cos 0) 
=cos (m + 1)0 + i sin (m + 1)0. 
This shows that the theorem holds for n = m + l if we assume it to 
hold for n = m. And the theorem holds for n = l.

gers n.

2.12. Exponential function. 
When x is real the infinite series 
1 
x2 
x3 
+x+-, +-, +· .. 
2. 
3. 
X 
X 
' 
fun 
converges for all x and the sum is denoted by e · e 18 a 
ction of 
real variable x defined for all x. This is called the exponential functio~ 
of a real variable x. 
For a complex variable z = x + iy, the exponential function of 
written as exp z, is defined by exp(x + iy) = ex(cos Y + i sin y). 
z, 
This definition agrees with the real exponential function when z is 
purely real. 
When z is purely real, y = O and exp z = ex(cos O + i sin 0), i.e. , 
exp x = ex. 
. 
When z is purely imaginary, x = 0 and exp z = (cos y + i sin Y), 
1.e., exp(iy) = cos y + i sin y. 
Since ex > O for all real x, ex ( cos y + i sin y) represents a complex 
number in polar form, ex being the modulus and y being an amplitude 
of exp z. 
Since ex =I=- 0 for any real number x, exp z is a non-zero complex 
number for any complex number z. 
Let u + iv be a non-zero complex number and let its polar represen-
tation be r(cos 0 + i sin 0). Since r is positive, log r is real and r can 
be expressed as r = e10g r. 
Therefore u + iv = 
e10g r ( cos 0 + i sin 0) 
exp (log r + i0). 
Thus when u + iv is a given non-zero complex number, there exists 
a complex number z = log r + i0 such that exp z = u + iv. This means 
that the range of the exponential function of z is the entire complex plane 
excluding the origin.

Note. A complex function f is said to be a periodic function on its 
domain D c (C if there exists a non-zero constant w such that for all 
integers n, f(z + nw) = J(z) (*) holds for all z E D. If no submultiple 
of w satisfies the relation (*), then w is said to be the period off. 
Let w be a non-zero complex number. Then there always exists a 
complex number z such that exp z = w. 
By the property 5, exp z = w => exp(z + 2n1ri) = w, where n is an 
integer. 
Thus for a non-zero complex number w there exist infinitely 1nany 
complex numbers z such that exp z = w.

2.13. Logarithmic function. 
Let z be a non-zero complex number. Then there always exists a 
complex number w such that exp w = z. 
w is said to be a logarithm of z. 
Again exp w = exp(w + 2n1ri), where n is an integer. This shows 
that if w is a logarithm of z, then w + 2n1ri is also a logarithm of z. 
This means that "logarithm of z" is a many-valued function of z. 
This is denoted by Log z = w + 2n1ri. 
Of the many values of logarithm of z, a particular one is called the 
principal logarithm of z and is denoted by log z. 
Since z is a non-zero complex number, z has a polar representation. 
Let z = r(cos 0 + i sin 0), -1r < 0 < 1r (a polar form with amp z). 
Let w = u + iv be a logarithm of z. Then exp w = z. This gives 
eu(cos v + i sin v) = r(cos (} + i sin 0). 
Therefore eu cos v = r cos 0, eu sin v = r sin 0. 
We have eu =rand cos v = cos 0, sin v = sin 0. 
These determine u = log rand v = 0 + 2n1r, where n is an integer. 
Therefore w = log r + i(0 + 2n1r), -1r < 0 < 1r 
i.e., Log z = log r + i(0 + 2n1r) 
= log I z I +i(arg z + 2n1r). 
The principal logarithm of z, denoted by log z, corresponds ton= 0. 
Therefore log z 
log r + i0, -1r < 0 < 1r 
= 
log I z I +i arg z.

9. cosh z and sinh z are periodic functions of period 21ri, tanh 
periodic function of period 1ri. 
Proof. cosh(z + 2k1ri) = cosh z cosh(2k1ri) + sinh z sinh(2k1ri) 
= cosh z cos(2k1r) + i sinh z sin(2k1r) 
= cosh z, if k be an integer. 
sinh(z + 2k1ri) = sinh z cosh(2k1ri) + cosh z sinh(2k1ri) 
= sinh z cos(2k1r) + i cosh z sin(2k1r) 
= sinh z, if k be an integer. 
89

Z IS a 
It follows that cosh z and sinh z are periodic functions of period 21ri. 
cosh(z + k1ri) 
cosh z cos(k1r) + i sinh z sin(k1r) 
(-I)k cosh z, if k be an integer. 
sinh(z + k1ri) 
sinh z cos( k1r) + i cosh z sin( k1r) 
(-l)k sinh z, if k be an integer. 
Therefore tanh(z + k1ri) = tanh z, for all integers k and so tanh z is 
a periodic function of period 1ri.

2.17. Inverse functions.

1. Let z be a given complex number and w oe a complex number such 
that sin w = z. Then w is said to be an inverse sine of z. 
sin w = z => cos w = ±✓1 - z2 • 
Then exp(iw) = iz ± ✓1 - z2 
or, w = -i Log(iz ± ✓1 - z2). 
Since Log z is a multiple-valued function of z, w is a multiple-valued 
function. The values of w are denoted by Sin-1 z. 
The principal value of w is obtained by choosing cos w = ✓1 - z2 
(the principal square root of 1 - z2 ) and taking the principal logarithm. 
It is denoted by sin-1 z. 
Therefore Sin-1z = -i Log(iz ± Jl - z2 ) 
and 
sin-1 z = -i log(iz + Jl - z2).

3. INTEGERS 
3.1. Natural numbers. 
The set N consiting of numbers 1, 2, 3, ... is called the set of all 
natural numbers. The well ordering property of the set N states that 
every non-empty subset of N contains a least element. 
This means that if S be a non-empty subset of N, there is some natural 
number a in S such that a < x for all x in S. 
3.1.1. Principle of induction. 
Let S be a subset of N with the properties -
(i) 1 belongs to S, and 
(ii) whenever a natural number k belongs to S, then k + I belongs 
to S. 
Then S = N. 
Proof. Let T be the set of all those natural numbers which are not in S. 
The theorem will be proved if we can prove that Tis an empty set. 
Let us assume that Tis a non-empty set. Then by the well ordering 
property T possesses a least element, say m. Since 1 E S, m > 1 and so 
m - 1 is a natural number. Again since m is the least element in T, m - 1 
is not in T and so m - 1 is in S. 
Since m - 1 is in S, 
by (ii) (m - 1) + 1 is in S, i.e., mis in S which 
is a contradiction. 
Therefore our assumption is wrong and T is empty and the theorem 
is proved. 
Theorem 3.1.2. Let En be a statement involving a natural number n. 
If 
. 
(i) 
E1 is true, and 
(ii) Ek+I is true whenever Ek is true, where k is a natural number, 
then En is true for all natural numbers. 
Proof. Let S be the set of those natural numbers n for which the state-
ment En is true. 

106 
HIGHER ALGEBRA 
Then S has the properties -
(i) 
1 ES, and 
(ii) 
k + 1 ES whenever k ES. 
Then by the principle of induction S = N. 
Thus En is true for all n E N. 
Note. To establish a theorem (or a proposition) involving natural num. 
hers by the principle of induction, both the conditions (i) and (ii) must 
be established. 
The condition (i) is called the basis of induction and the assumption 
made in the condition (ii) is called the induction hypothesis. 
Worked Examples.

1 + 2 + • • • + n = n(n2+1) for all natural numbers n.

This shows that the statement is true for the natural number k + 1 if 
it is true for k. 
By the principle of induction, the statement is true for all natural 
numbers n.

3.1.3. Second principle of induction. 
Let S be a subset of N such that (i) 1 ES, 
and (ii) if { 1, 2, .. . , k} c S, then k + 1 E S. 
Then S = N. 
Proof. Let T = N - S. We prove that T = </>. If not, T being a non-
empty subset of N must have a least element, say m, by the well ordering 
property of N. 
Since 1 E S, m =I- 1. 
Therefore m > 1. 
By the choice of m, all natural numbers less than m belongs to s. 
Hence 1, 2, ... , m - 1 E S. 
By (ii) 
m E S, a contradiction. 
This proves T = (/) and therefore S = N.

3.2. Integers. 
The set of all integers, denoted by Z, consists of whole nuµibers 
0, ±1, ±2, ±3,... 
. .. The set of all positive integers (a proper subset 
of Z) is identified with the set N. We shall use the properties and prin-
ciples of N in connection with the proof of any theorem about positive 
integers. 
Theorem 3.2.1. Division algorithm. 
Given integers a and b with b > 0, there exist unique integers q and 
r such that 
a = bq + r, where O < r < b. 
Proof. Let us consider the subset of integers 
S = { a - bx : x E Z, a - bx > 0}. 
First we show that S is non-empty. 
Since b > l, I a I b > I a I- Therefore a+ I a I b > a+ I a I> 0. 
This shows that a - b(-lal) ES and therefore Sis non-empty. 
Since Sis a non-empty set of non-negative integers, either 
(i) S contains O as its least element, or 
(ii) S contains a smallest positive inetger as its least element by the 
well ordering property of the set N. 
In either case, we call it r. Therefore there exists an integer q such 
that a - bq = r, r > 0. 
We assert that r < b. Because if r > b, then 
a - (q + l)b = (a - qb) - b = r - b > 0. 
This shows that a - ( q + 1 )b belongs to S and also a - ( q + l )b = 
r - b < r. This leads to a contradiction to the fact that r is the least 
element in S. 
Hence r < b and consequently, a = bq + r where O < r < b. 
In order to establish uniqueness of q and r, let us suppose that a 
has two representations: 
a = bq + r, a = bq1 + r1 where O < r < b, 
0 < T1 < b.

Worked Examples. 
1. Prove that the product of any m consecutive integers is divisible by 
m. 
Let the consecutive integers be c, c + I, c + 2, ... , c + (m - 1). 
By division algorithm, there exist integers q and r such that c = 
mq + r, 
0 < r < m. 
When r = 0, c = mq and therefore 
m I c; 
when r = 1, c + (m - I) = m(q + 1) and therefore 
m I c + (m - 1); 
when r = 2, c + m - 2 = m(q + 1) and therefore 
m I c + (m - 2); 
when r = m-1, c+ 1 = m(q+ 1) and therefore 
m I c+ I. 
Therefore whatever integer r may be, m divides one of the integers 
c, c+l, . .. , c+(m-1) and it follows that the product c(c+I)(c+2) ... (c+ 
m - 1) is always divisible by m. 
2. Use division algorithm to prove that the square of an odd integer is 
of the form Bk+ 1, where k is an integer. 
By division algorithm every integer, upon division by 4, leaves one 
of the remainders 0, 1, 2, 3. Therefore any integer is one of the forms 
4q, 4q + 1, 4q + 2, 4q + 3, where q is an integer. 
Odd integers are of the forms 4q + 1, 4q + 3. 
Now (4q + 1)2 = B(2q2 + q) + l is of the form Bk+ 1, 
( 4q + 3) 2 = 8(2q2 + 3q + l) + 1 is of the form Bk + I. 
Hence the square of an odd integer is of the form Bk + I. 
Definition. If a and b are integers then an integer d is said to be a 
common divisor of a and b if d I a as well as d I b. 
Since 1 is a divisor of every integer, 1 is a common divisor of a and 
b. Therefore, for an arbitrary pair of integers a, b there exists always a 
common divisor. 
If both of a and b be O then each integer is a common divisor of a and 
b. But if at least one of a and b is non-zero there is only a finite number 
of positive common divisors. Of these positive common divisors, there

1, 2, 3, 4, 6, 12 and those of -18 are 1, 2, 3, 6, 9, 18. Therefore the positive 
common divisors are 1, 2, 3, 6 and gcd(12, -18) = 6.

Theorem 3.2.5. If k be a positive integer, gcd(ka, kb) = k.gcd(a, b).

8. Prove that the product of any three consecutive integers is divisible 
by 6. 
By division algorithm, any integer, upon division by 3, leaves one 
of the remainders 0, 1, 2. Therefore any integer n is one of the forms 
3k,3k + 1,3k + 2. 
When n = 3k, n is divisible by 3. 
When n = 3k + 1, n + 2 is divisible by 3. 
When n = 3k + 2, n + 1 is divisible by 3. 
It follows that for any integer n, n(n + l)(n + 2) is divisible by 3. 
Again, the product of two consecutive integers is divisible by 2. 
Therefore 2 I n(n + l)(n + 2) and 3 I n(n + l)(n + 2). 
Since gcd(2, 3) = 1, it follows that 2.3 I n(n + l)(n + 2), i.e., 6 I 
n(n + l)(n + 2).

3.2.11. Euclidean algorithm. 
Euclidean algorithm is an efficient method of finding the greatest 
common divisor of two given integers. The method involves repeated 
application of the division algorithm. 
Let a and b be two integers whose g .c.d. is required. 
Since gcd( a, b) = gcd( I a I, I b I), it is enough to assume that a and b 
are positive integers. Without loss of generality, we assume a> b > 0. 
By division algorithm, a = bq1 + r1 where 0 < r1 < b. 
ff it happens that r 1 = 0, then b I a and gcd( a, b) = b. 
If r1 f:. 0, then by division algorithm, b = r1 q2 + r2 where 0 < r2 < r 1. 
If r2 = 0, the process stops. If r2 f:. 0, then by division algorithm, 
r = r2q3 + r3 where 0 < r3 < r2. 
The process continues until some zero remainder appears. This must 
happen because the remainders r1, r2, r3, ... form a decreasing sequence 
of integers and since r 1 < b, the sequence contains at most b non-negative 
integers. 
Let us assume that r n+ 1 = 0 and r n is the last non-zero remainder. 
We have the following relations

Definition. Let a1, a2, ... , an be integers different from zero. An integer 
b is said to be a common multiple of a 1, a2 , ..• , an if ai I b for i 
1,2, ... ,n. 
In fact, common multiples do exist. 
O is a common multiple of 
a1, a2, ... , an. The products a1a2 . .. an and --a1a2 ... an are both com-
mon multiples of a1, a2, ... , an and one of these is positive. By the well 
ordering property of the set N, the set of all positive common multiples 
contains a least element which is of special interest. 
Definition. Least common multiple. 
Let a1 , a2, ... , an be integers different from zero. The least positive 
common multiple is said to be the least common multiple (lcm) of the 
integers a1, a2, ... , an and it is denoted by [a1, a2, ... , an]-
For example, the lcm of 2, 3, 6 is 6; the lcm of -2, -3, -6 is 6; the 
lcm of -2, -6, 10 is 30. 
Theorem 3.2.12. 
If h be any common multiple of the integers 
a1, a2, ... , an, none of which is zero, then [a1, a2, ... , an] I h. 
Proof. Let m be the lcm of a1, a2, ... , an. By division algorithm, there 
exist integers q and r such that h = mq + r, where O < r < m. 
For each i = 1, 2, ... , n, 
ai I h and ai I m and therefore ai I r. 
This shows that r is a common multiple. If r > 0 then r becomes 
a positive common multiple less than the least common multiple m, a 
contradiction. 
Therefore r = 0 and consequently, m is a divisor of h. 
This completes the proof. 
Note. If m = [a1, a2 , •••,an] then the common multiples of the integers 
a1,a2, ... ,an is the set {O,±m,±2m,···}.

Theorem 3.2.14. If a and bare integers different from zero, then 
[a,b](a,b) =I ab I, 
where [a, b] = the lcm of a and b; (a, b) = the gcd of a and b. 
Proof. First we assume that a and b are positive integers. 
Case 1. Let (a, b) = 1.

Then b I pa. 
Since ( a, b) = 1, it follows that b I p. Therefore b < p, ba < pa. 
Again, ba being a common multiple of a and b cannot be less than 
[a, b], i.e., cannot be less than pa, so that ba = pa. 
Thus [a, b] = ab and this gives [a, b](a, b) = ab.

In the general case, let a, b be non-zero integers, positive or negative.

Linear Diophantine equation. 
An equation in one or more unknowns which is to be solved in integei:s 
is said to be a Diophantine equation, named after the Greek 111athen1a.ti-
cian Diophantus, who initiated the study of such problen1s. 
, 
A linear Diophantine equation of the fonn ax+ by = c may have 111an) 
solutions in integers or may not have even a single solution.

3.3. Prime numbers. 
An integer p > 1 is said to be a prime number, or simply a prime, if 
its only positive divisors are 1 and p. 
An integer> 1 which is not a prime is said to be a composite number. 
The integers 2, 3, 5, 7, 11, ... are prime numbers, while the integers 
4, 6, 8, 9, ... are composite numbers. 
The integer 1 is regarded as neither prime nor composite. 
2 is the only even prime number. All other prime numbers are neces-
sarily odd. 
Theorem 3.3.1. Hp be a prime number and 1 <a< p, then pis priDle 
to a. 
Proof. Let d = gcd(a,p). Then d I a and d Ip. 
Since pis a prime and d Ip, either d = p or d = 1. 
. 
But since a< p and d I a, d cannot be p. Therefore d = 1 and P 15 
prime to a. 
Theorem 3.3.2. If p be a prime number and a is an integer> P SUch 
that p is not a divisor of a, then p is prime to a. 
Proof. Let d = gcd(a,p). Then d I a and d Ip. 

INTEGERS 
125 
Since p is a prime and d I p, either d = p or d = 1. 
But d -/= p since p is not a divisor of a. Therefore d = I and p is prime 
to a. 
Theorem 3.3.3. If p be a prime number and a is an integer > p such 
that p is a divisor of a, then gcd( a, p) = p. 
Proof. Since pis a divisor of a, a= pk where k is an integer. 
Hence gcd(a,p) = gcd(pk,p) = p.gcd(k, 1) = p. 
Theorem 3.3.4. If p be a prime number and p I ab, then either p I a or 
p I b. 
Proof. If p I a then the theorem is done. 
If pis not a divisor of a then gcd(a,p) = 1, since 1 and pare the only 
divisors of p. 
Since gcd( a, p) = 1, there exist integers u and v such that au+ pv = l. 
Then abu + pbv = b. 
Now p I ab and p I pb => p I (ab)u + (pb)v, since u and v are integers. 
That is, p I b. 
This completes the proof. 
Corollary. If p be a prime and p I a1 a2 .•• an, then p I ak for some k 
where 1 < k < n . 
Proof. If p I a1 we need not go further. If p is not a divisor of a1 then by 
the theorem, p I a2a3 ... an. 
If pis not a divisor of a2 then p I a3a4 ... an. Proceeding in a similar 
manner, in a finite number of steps we arrive at the desired result. 
Theorem 3.3.5. A composite number has at least one prime divisor. 
Proof. Let n be a composite number. Since n is not a prime, it has a 
positive divisor other than 1 and n. 
Let S be the set of those positive divisors of n which are different 
from 1 and n. Then S is non-empty. By the well ordering property of 
the set N, S contains a least element, say d. Then 1 < d < n. 
We prove that dis a prime. 
If d be not a prime then d has a divisor d' other than d and 1; and 
1 < d' < d < n. But d' I d and d In=> d' I n. Therefore d' E Sand this 
contradicts that d is the least element of S. 
Therefore d is a prime and the theorem is done.

Worked Examples. 
1. Prove that for n > 3, the integers n, n + 2, n + 4 cannot be all prirnes. 
Any positive integer n is one of the forms 3k, 3k + 1, 3k + 2, where k 
is a positive integer. 
If n = 3k then n is not a prime. 
If n = 3k + 1 then n + 2 = 3( k + l) and it is not a prime. 
If n = 3k + 2 then n + 4 = 3(k + 2) and it is not a prime. 
Thus in any case, the integers n, n + 2, n + 4 are not all primes. 
2. p is a positive integer and p, 2p + l, 4p + 1 are primes. Find p. 
pis one of the forms 3k, 3k + 1, 3k + 2, where k is an integer. 
If p = 3k + 1 then 2p + 1 = 6k + 3 = 3(2k + 1) and it is not a prime. 
If p = 3k + 2 then 4p+ 1 = 12k + 9 = 3(4k + 3) and it is not a prime. 
The only conclusion is, p = 3k. Since p is a prime, k = 1. So p == 3. 
3. If p > q > 5 and p, q are both primes, prove that 24 I (p2 - q2). 
Since p and q are primes > 3, p and q are of the form 3k + l or 3k + 2, 
where k is an integer. 
If both p and q are either of the forms 3k + 1 or 3k + 2, then 3 I (p- q). 
If one of p and q is of the form 3k + 1 and the other is of the form 
3k + 2, them 3 I (p + q). 
Thus in any case, 3 I (p2 - q2 ) . 
Since p and q are odd primes, p and q are of the form 4k+ 1 or 4k+3, 
where k is an integer. 
If both p and q are of the form 4k + 1, then 2 I (p + q) and 4 I (p-q). 
If both p and q are of the form 4k + 3, then 2 I (p + q) and 4 I (p-q). 
If one of p and q is of the form 4k + 1 and the other is of the form 
4k + 3, then 4 I (p + q) and 2 I (p - q). 
Thus in any case, 8 I (p2 -
q2). 
Since 3 and 8 are prime to each other, 24 I (p2 - q2). 
4. If p and p 2 + 8 are both prime numbers, prove that p = 3. 
Any integer p is one of the forms 3k, 3k + 1, 3k + 2, where k is an 
integer. 
If p = 3k + l, then p
2 + 8 = 3(3k2 + 2k + 3). Since p2 + 8 is a prillle~ 
3k2 + 2k + 3 must be 1 for some integer k and in that case p2 + 8 nius 
be 3. 
But for no integer k, 3k2 + 2k + 3 can be 1 and for no integer k, P2 +B 
can be 3. Therefore p = 3k + 1 is an impossibility.

By similar arguments, p = 3k + 2 is an impossibility. 
Therefore P = 3k, where k is an integer. Since pis a prime, k must 
be 1 and therefore p = 3. 
5. If 2n - 1 be a prime, prove that n is a prime. 
Let n be composite. Then n = p.q where p and q are integers each 
greater than 1. 
2n - 1 = 2Pq - 1 = (2P - 1)(2P(q-l) + 2P(q-2) + · · · + 2P + 1). 
Each factor on the right is evidently greater than 1 and therefore 
2n - 1 is composite. 
Contrapositively, 2n - 1 is a prime implies n is a prime. 
6. Prove that n4 + 4n is a composite number for all n > l. 
Case 1. Let n be even. 
Then n 4 + 4n is divisible by 4 and so it is a composite number. 
Case 2. Let n be odd and n = 2k + 1, where k is a natural number. 
Then n4 + 4n = n4 + 4.42k = n4 + 4a4 , where a = 2k 
= (n2 +2a2)2-(2an)2 = (n2 +2an+2a2)(n2 -2an+2a2 ). 
(n2 +2an+2a2 ) = (n+a) 2 +a2 and (n2 -2an+2a2 ) = (n-a) 2 +a2• 
Since a is a positive integer> 1, (n+a)2+a2 > 1 and (n-a) 2+a2 > 1. 
Consequently, n4 + 4n is a composite number when n is odd. 
Hence n4 + 4n is a composite number for all n > l.

Theorem 3.3.6. (Fundamental theorem of Arithmetic} 
Any positive integer is either 1, or a prime, or it can be expreesed as 
a product of primes, the representation being unique except for the order 
of the prime factors. 
Proof. Let n be a positive integer. Either n = l or n > l. Let P(n) be 
the statement that n( > 1) is either a prime, or it can be expressed as a 
product of primes. 
P(2) is true, since 2 is a prime. 
Let us assume that P( n) is true for all n, where n is a positive integer 
such that 2 < n < k. 
If k + l be itself a prime then P( k + l) is true and by the second 
principle of induction, P(n) is true for all positive integers n > I. 
If k + l be not a prime then it is a composite number. Let k + 1 = rs 
where r, s are integers with 2 < r < k + l, 2 < s < k + l. 
By induction hypothesis, P(r) and P(s) are both true. Then 
r = P1P2 .. ·Pi where P1,P2, ... ,Pi are primes, i > 1; 
s = q1q2 •.. Qj where q1 , q2, ... , qi are primes, j > 1. 
Thus k + l is expressed as the product of primes and P(k + 1) is 
proved to be true. By the second principle of induction p( n) is true for 
all positive integers n > l. 
Hence the first part of the theorem is established. 
In order to prove uniqueness of the representation, let us assume that 
n = P1P2 • • • Pk = Q1 Q2 . . • Qm, where Pi and qi are all primes. 
Since P1 I n, it follows that P1 I Q1 q2 ... qm. 
Since P1 is a prime, P1 I qr for some r where 1 < r < m. But since Pi 
and qr are both primes, P1 = Qr. 
We obtain 
P2P3 •••Pk= Q1Q2 • • • qr-lqr+l ... qm. 
We repeat the argument with P2 and obtain p2 = q8 for some s, where 
1 < s < m, s =/= r. Then 
p3p4 •··Pk= Q1q2 · · · qr-lqr+l · · · Qs-lQs+l ... Qm• 
If k < m, then after k steps the left hand side reduces to 1 and the 
right hand side becomes the product of m - k 
q's, each of which is a 
prime. This cannot happen. Therefore k > m. 
If k > m, then after m steps the right hand side reduces to 1 and 
the left hand side becomes the product of k - m p's, each of which is a 
prime. This cannot happen. Therefore k < m. 
Hence k = m and the products P1P2 ... Pm and q1 q2 .• • qk give the 
same representation except for the order of the factors.

Thus n(> 1) is expressed as the product of a number of primes, the 
representation being unique except for the order of the factors. 
Note. In the application of the fundamental theorem we write any inte-
ger n(> 1) in the form, called the canonical form, 
n = Pl 01 P2 a2 ••• Pr Or' 
where the primes Pi are distinct with p1 < p2 < • • • < Pr and the expo-
nents ai are positive. 
An integer is said to be square-free if no ai in the canonical form of 
n is greater than 1. 
To illustrate the representation, let us take n = 3150, 210. 
3150 = 2.3.3.5.5.7 = 2.32.52.7. 210 = 2.3.5.7. 210 is square free.

Note. If we define binary operations o and * on the set N by 
a o b = the g.c.d. of a and b, 
a* b = the l.c.m. of a and b for a, b E N 
131 
then the part (i) of the theorem states that a o (b*c) = (a ob)* (a o c) for 
a, b EN and the part (ii) of the theorem states that a*(boc) = (a*b)o(a*c) 
for a, b EN. 
These establish that the operation o is distributive over the operation 
* and the operation* is distributive over the operation o. 
Worked Examples. 
1. If 2n + 1 is an odd prime for some integer n, prove that n is a power 
of 2. 
If n is odd, 2n + 1 is divisible by 2 + 1 and therefore 2n + 1 is not a 
prime. Son is even.

Then n = 2k .p, where pis an odd integer. 
If p > 1, 2n + 1 = 22k ·P + 1 = (22k )P + 1 and it is divisible by 22k + 1, 
since p is odd. This contradicts that 2n + 1 is a prime. 
Consequently, p = 1 and n = 2k. 
2. If p be a prime, show that ..,/P is not a rational number. 
Since p is a prime, p is an integer > 2 and therefore ../P > I. 
Let ..,/P be a rational number. Then ..,/P = 1;: for some natural num-
bers m, n. We assert that m > 1 and n > I, because 
m = I and n = l => p = 12 = 1, a contradiction 
m > 1 and n = 1 => p = m. m and therefore p is not a prime, a 
contradiction 
m = 1 and n > l => ..,/P < 1, a contradiction. 
Therefore m > 1 and n > I. We also have pn2 = m 2 • The number 
of primes in the factorisation of m being unique by the fundamental 
theorem of arithmetic, it follows that the number of primes ( counting 
multiplicity) in the factorisation of m 2 is always even. 
Similarly, the number of primes in the factorisation of n 2 is also even. 
Therefore the number of primes in the factorisation of pn 2 is odd ( since 
Pis a prime). 
Since pn2 = m 2 , it appears that the same integer m 2 is expressed as 
the product of an odd number of primes in one representation and as the 
product of an even number of primes in another representation. 
This contradicts uniqueness of the number of prime factors in the 
decompostion. We conclude that /P is not a rational number.

Theorem 3.3.8. (Euclid) The number of primes is infinite. 
Proof. We prove the theorem by contradiction. . 
. 
Let us suppose that the number of primes is ~nite ao<l ~et P be the 
greatest prime. We write the primes 2, 3, 5, 7, • • • in succession and p is 
the last in the enumeration. 
The product 2.3.5 ... p in which every prime appears only once is 
divisible by each prime and therefore the number (2.3.5 · · · P) + 1 is not 
divisible by any of the primes 2, 3, 5, ... , p. 
Hence this number is either itself a prime, or being a composite num-
ber, is divisible by a prime number greater than p. In both the cases 
p fails to be the greatest prime and therefore the number of primes is 
infinite. 
Note. Although the number of primes is infinite, there are arbitrarily 
large gaps in the sequence of primes. For every positive integer k, there 
exist k consecutive composite numbers. To be explicit, each of the k 
consecutive integers 
(k + 1)! + 2, (k + 1)! + 3, ... , (k + 1)! + (k + 1) 
is composite, because (k + 1)! + r is divisible by r if 2 < r < k + 1. 
This indicates that the primes are irregularly spaced in the sequence 
of positive integers. The number of primes less than a positive integer 
x is denoted by 1r(x). No simple formula for determining 1r(x) has yet 
been found. 
Test for primality. 
If a positive integer a be composite, then a = be for integers b, c 
satisfying 1 < b < a, 1 < e < a. Let b < e. Then b2 < be = a and this 
implies b < y'a. 
-
Since b > I, b has at least one prime divisor p and p < b < ..fa,. 
In testing primality of a positive integer n, it is sufficient to divide n 
by primes not exceeding ..Jii,. 
Greek mathematician, Eratosthenes (276 - 494 B.C.) utilised this con· 
cept to find all primes less than a given positive integer n. His device 
is called the "seive of Eratosthenes" which consists in writing all inte-
gers from 2 to n in natural order and then striking out all multiples 
2p, 3p, 4p, 5p, ... of all primes p < y'n. The integers that are left in the 
list ( survived the seive ) are primes. 
For example, in order to determine all primes < 30 the "sieve" 
method is applied by striking all multiples of 2 3 5 from' the table of 
integers from 2 to 30, since 5 is the largest prime' J J3o.

Distribution of primes. 
According to Divison algorithm, all odd positive integers fall into two 
progressions - one containing positive integers of the form 4n + I and 
the other containing positive integers of the form 4n + 3, where n EN. 
It is natural to ask whether the infinitude of primes ultimately fall 
into one particular progression or they are infinitely distributed in both 
the progressions. 
In this respect a more general theorem has been established by Dirich-
let which states that - if a and b are positive integers relatively prime 
then the arithmetic progression a + nb ( n E N) contains infinitely many 
prunes. 
The proof of the general theorem requires analytic method and as 
such it is beyond the scope of the book. In special cases the proofs can 
be carried along the same line as in Euclid's theorem. We consider one 
such. 
Theorem 3.3.9.(Dirichlet) There are infinitely many primes of the 
form 4n -1. 
Proof. We assume that there are only a finite number of primes of the 
form 4n - 1. Let p be the largest prime of that form. 
Let us consider the positive integer N = (22 .3.5 ... p) - 1. The product 
3.5 ... p contains all odd primes < p as divisors. 
Since N is of the form 4n - 1 and N > p, N cannot be a prime, 
because by assumption, p is the largest prime of the form 4n - l. 
As N is composite, N has prime divisors and they are all greater than 
p. All of Them cannot be of the form 4n + 1, because the product of two 
numbers of the form 4n + l is of the same form. 
Therefore some prime divisor of N must be of the form 4n - 1 and 
clearly that prime number is greater than p and this contradicts our 
assumption that p is the largest prime of the form 4n - 1. 
The contradiction proves the theorem.

Theorem 3.3.11. The total number of positive divisors of a positive 
integer n is odd if and only if n is a perfect square. 
Proof. Let n( > 1) be a perfect square and let the canonical form of n be

factors 01 + 1, 02 + 1, . . • , Or + 1 must be odd. Consequently, each of 
01, a 2, ... , Or must be even and n is therefore a perfect square. 
This completes the proof.

3.4. Congruence. 
Karl Friedrich Gauss (1777-1855), a celebrated German mathemati-
cian, introduced the concept of congruence which laid the foundation of 
modern theory of numbers. 
Definition. Let m be a fixed positive integer. Two integers a and bare 
said to be congruent modulo m if a - b is divisible by m. symbolically 
this is expressed as a = b (mod m). 
To illustrate, let m = 3. It is easy to-verify that 
1 = 
4 (mod 3), -2 = 
1 (mod 3), 6 = 
0 (mod 3), 35 = 2 (mod 3). 
When a - b is not divisible by m, a is said to be incongruent to b 
modulo m. It is expressed as a¢ b (mod m). 
For example, 1 ¢ 5 (mod 3), -2 ¢ 2 (mod 3). 
Note. When m = 1, every two integers are congruent modulo m and 
this case is not so useful and interesting. Therefore m is usually taken 
to be a positive integer greater than 1. 
Theorem 3.4.1. For any two integers a and b, a= b (mod m) if and 
only if a and b leave the same remainder when divided by m. 
Proof. Let r be the remainder when a is divided by m. Then there exists 
some integer q such that a = qm + r, 0 < r < m. 
Since a= b (mod m), a - b = km where k is an integer. 
Therefore b = a - km = (qm + r) - km 
= (q-k)m+r 
and this shows that b leaves the same remainder r. 
Conversely, let r be the same remainder when a and bare divided by m. 
Then a = q1 m + r , b = q2m + r, where Q1, Q2 are integers and O < r < m. 
Therefore (a - b) = (q1 - q2)m, i.e., m I a - b and this proves that 
a= b (mod m). 
To illustrate, let m = 5. Since 21 = 4.5 + 1 and -14 = -3.5 + 1, 
21 and -14 leave the same remainder upon division by 5. Therefore 
21 = -14 (mod 5). 
Properties. 
1. a = a ( mod m). 
2. If a= b (mod m) then b = a (mod m). 
3. If a= b (mod m),b = c (mod m) then a= c (mod m). 

142 
HIGHER ALGEBRA 
4. H a = b ( mod m) then for any integer c 
a + c = b + c (mod m) 
ae = be (mod m). 
5. li a = b ( mod m) and e = d ( mod m) then 
a + e = b + d (mod m) 
ae = bd (mod m). 
6. If a= b (mod m) and dim, d > 0, then a= b (mod d). 
Proofs of properties 1 - 4 and 6 are immediate. 
Proof. 5. a= b (mod m)::::} a - b = km and 
e = d (mod m)::::} e - d = lm, where k, l are integers. 
( a + e) - (b + d) = ( k + l)m. 
Therefore a + e = b + d ( mod m) since k + l is an integer. 
By property 4, 
a - b (mod m)⇒ ae = be (mod m) and 
e = d (mod m)⇒ be= bd (mod m). 
Therefore a= b (mod m) and e = d (mod m)::::} ae = bd (mod m). 
Definition. If a = b ( mod m) then b is said to be a residue of a modulo 
m. 
By division algorithm there exist integers q and r satisfying a = qm+r 
with 0 < r < m - l. 
Since a - r = qm, a = r ( mod m) and this shows that r is a residue 
of a modulo m. r is said to be the least non-negative residue of a modulo 
m. 
Let a be an arbitrary integer. Upon division by m, a leaves one and 
only one of the integers 0, 1, 2, ... , m - 1 as the remainder. 
Therefore whatever the integer a may be, the least non-negative 
residue of a is one and only one of 0, 1, 2, . . . , m - 1. 
The whole set of integers is divided into m distinct and disjoint sub-
sets, called the residue classes modulo m, denoted by O, I, 2, ... , m ..:1 
and defined by 
0 = {0, ±m, ±2m, . .. } 
I = {1, 1 ± m, 1 ± 2m, ... } 
2 = {2, 2 ± m, 2 ± 2m, . .. } 
m -1 = {m - 1, (m - 1) ± m, (m - 1) ± 2m, ... }. 

INTEGERS 
143 
Any two integers in a residue class are congruent modulo m and any 
two integers belonging to two different residue classes are incongruent 
modulo m. 
Theorem 3.4.2. If a= b (mod m) then an= bn (mod m) for all positive 
integers n. 
Proof. We use the principle of induction to prove the theorem. 
The theorem is true for n = 1. 
Let us assume that the theorem is true for some positive integer k. 
Then ak = bk (mod m). 
Nowak= bk (mod m) and a= b (mod m) together imply that 
ak .a= bk .b (mod m), i.e., ak+l = bk+l (mod m). 
This shows that the thoerem is true for the positive integer k + l if 
we assume it to be true fork. 
By the principle of induction, the theorem is true for all positive 
integers n. 
Note. The converse of the theorem fails to hold. 
ak = bk (mod m) does not necessarily imply a= b (mod m). 
For example, 92 = 72 (mod 8) but 9 ¢ 7 (mod 8) 
43 = 73 (mod 9) but 4 ¢ 7 (mod 9).

3.4. 7. Divisibility tests. 
1. Let n = amlom + am-1lom-l + ... + a2102 + a110 + ao where ak are 
integers and O < ak < 9, k = 0, 1, ... , m be the decimal representation of 
a positive integer n.

(i) n is divisible by 2 if and only if a0 is divisible by 2; 
(ii) n is divisible by 9 if and only if Sis divisible by 9; 
(iii) n is divisible by 11 if and only if Tis divisible by 11. 
Proof. Let us consider the polynomial 
f(x) = amxm + am-1Xm-l + · · · + a1x + ao, 
(i) We have 10 = 0 (mod 2). 
Therefore /(10) = f(O) (mod 2). 
But /(10) = n 
and f (O) = ao. 
Therefore n - ao is divisible by 2. 
Hence n is divisible by 2 if and only if a0 is divisible by 2. 
(ii) We have 10 = 1 (mod 9). 
Therefore /(10) = /(1) (mod 9). 
But f (IO) = n 
and f (1) = S. 
Therefore n = S ( mod 9). 
This proves that N - Sis divisible by 9. 
Hence n is divisible by 9 if and only if Sis divisible by 9. 
(iii) We have 10 = -1 (mod 11). 
Therefore /(10) = f(-1) (mod 11). 
But /(10) = n 
and J(-1) = T. 
Therefore n = T (mod 11). 
This proves that n - Tis divisible by 11. 
Hence n is divisible by 11 if and only if Tis divisible by 11. 
For example, 35078571 is divisible by 9 since the sum of the digits 
3 + 5 + O + 7 + 8 + 5 + 7 + 1(= 36) is divisible by 9. 
It is also divisible by 11 because the sum 
1- 7 + 5 - 8 + 7 - O + 5 - 3(= 0) is divisible by 11. 
The number 23572 is divisible by 2, since the integer ao in the units 
Place is 2 which is divisible by 2. It is not divisible by 9, since the sum 
2 + 3 + 5 + 7 + 2( = 19) is not divisible by 9. It is not divisible by 11, 
since the sum 2 - 7 + 5 - 3 + 2(= -1) is not divisible by 11.

3.4.8. Linear congruence. 
Let /(x) = a0xn + a1xn-l + · • • + an(n > 1) be a polynomial with 
integer coefficients ao, a1 , ... , an with ao ~ 0 ( mod m). Then / ( x) = 0 
(mod m) is said to be a polynomial congruence (mod m) of degree n. 
If there exists an integer x0 such that J(xo) = 0 (mod m), then x0 is 
said to be a solution of the congruence. 
By earlier theorems, if x1 be any integer satisfying x1 -
xo (mod 
m), then we also have /(x1) -
0 (mod m), showing that x1 is another 
solution of the congruence. 
Thus if one solution be found then infinitely many solutions can be 
obtained, but all these solutions belong to the same xa-residue class mod-
ulo m and they are not counted as different solutions. 
Two solutions x1, x 2 of f ( x) = 0 ( mod m) are said to be distinct 
solutions if x1 ~ x 2 ( mod m). 
Therefore, by the number of solutions of a congruence ( mod m) we 
mean the number of solutions incongruent in pairs. 
For example, let us consider the congruence x2 = 1 (mod 8). x = 1 

148 
HIGHER ALGEBRA 
is a solution of the congruence and all soluti~ns congruent to 1 (tnod S) 
i.e., x = 1 + Bk, k being an integer, are solutions of the congruence. 
1 
x = 3 is a solution of the congruence and all solutions congruent 
to 3 (mod 8), i.e., x = 3 + 8k, k being an integer are solutions of the 
congruence. 
Similarly, x = 5, x = 7 are solutions of the congruence. 
These four solutions of the congruence are distinct, because no two 
of the solutions are congruent modulo 8. 
There cannot be more than m distinct solutions of the congruence 
since there are only m different residue classes. If m is small it is an eas; 
job to find all the distinct solutions by direct substitution x = 1, x == 2 , 
,x=m-1. 
There are many points of difference between a polynomial congruence 
modulo a positive integer m > 1 and the polynomial equation over the 
field of complex numbers. 
A congruence may have no solution. For example, the congruence 
x 2 = 3 (mod 5) has no solution which can be established by directly 
verifying that none of x = 0, x = 1, x = 2, x = 3, x = 4 satisfies the 
congruence. In contrast, a polynomial equation has always a solution. 
A congruence may have more distinct solutions than its degree. For 
example, the congruence x2 = 1 (mod 8) has four distinct solutions 
x = 1,x = 3, x = 5, x = 7. In contrast, a polynomial equation of degree 
m over the complex field has exactly m solutions. 
There is an explicit method of solving a congruence of any degree 
modulo a positive integer m > 1. Llust by substitution of each of the 
integers 1, 2, .. . , m - 1, in turn.] But there is no such explicit method 
for solving a polynomial equation of degree greater than 4. 
Definition. 
A polynomial congruence of degree 1 is said to be a linear congruence. 
The general form of a linear congruence modulo a positive integer m > 1 
is ax= b (mod m), where a¢ 0 (mod m ). 
An integer c is said to be a solution of the linear congruence ax :::: b 
(mod m) if ac = b (mod m).

congruence.

Theorem 3.4.10. If gcd(a, m) = 1, then the linear congruence ax= b 
(mod m) has a unique solution. 
Proof. Since gcd( a, m) = 1, there exist integers u and v such that au + 
mv = 1. Therefore a(bu) + m(bv) = b. This gives a(bu) = b (mod m). 
This shows that x = bu is a solution of the congruence ax = b ( mod 
m). 
Let x1, x2 be solutions of the congruence ax= b (mod m). 
Then ax1 = b (mod m) and ax2 = b (mod m). 
Therefore ax1 = ax2 (mod m) and this implies x 1 = x2 (mod m), 
since gcd(a, m) = 1. 
This proves that the congruence has a unique solution. 
Note. The solutions are x = bu+ ..\m, where ..\ = 0, ±1, ±2, ... and they 
all belong to one· and only one residue class modulo m.

System of linear congruences. 
Let us consider the linear congruences 
a1x = 
b1 (mod m1), a2x = 
b2 (mod m2), · · ·, arx = 
br (mod mr) 
and let us enquire if it be possible to have a simultaneous solution of 
the congruences. In that case each individual congruence must have a 
solution. 
Let gcd(ai, mi) = di for i = 1, 2, ... , r. Then di must be a divisor of 
bi for each i. 
Cancelling di from the ith equation, the system reduces to 
a~x = b~ (mod m~), a~x = b~ (mod m;), .. ·, a~x _ b~ (mod m~), 
where dia~ = ai, dib~ = bi, ... , dim~ = mi and gcd(a~, mD = 1 for 
i=l,2, ... ,r. 
Each individual congruence has a unique solution of the form xi = Ci 
(mod mi ). 
Thus the problem is reduced to one of finding a common solution of 
the system 
x = c1 (mod m1), x = c2 (mod m2), · · ·, x = Cr (mod mr), 
The kind of problems that can be reduced to a system of linear con-
gruences was found in Chinese literature as early as first century A.D . .In 
later periods such problems were also found in other countries. Because 
of their antiquity, this type of problem goes by. the name of "Chinese 
remainder theorem" . 
The method of congruence that is used to state the problem and to 
make the proof in a concise form was unknown to the ancients.

3.5. Phi function. 
Definition. The function ¢, called Euler's phi function (Euler's totient 
function), is defined for all positive integers by 
</>(1) = 1 and for n > 1, 
</>( n) = the number of positive integers less than n and prime to n. 
For example, let n = 8, 20. 
The positive integers less than 8 and prime to 6 are 1, 3, 5, 7. Therefore 
</>(8) = 4. 
The positive integers less than 20 and prime to 20 are 1, 3, 7, 9, ll, 
13, 17, 19. Therefore ¢(20) = 8. 
If p be a prime then every positive integer less than p is prime to P· 
Therefore <f>(p) = p - 1. 
Note. The function¢ is a number-theoretic function.

Theorem 3.5.1. The function¢ is a multiplicative function. That is, if 
m and n be relatively prime integers, then ¢( mn) = ¢( m )¢( n). 
First we prove the following lemmas. 
Lemma 1. a is prime to mn if and only if a is prime to m and a is prime 
ton. 
Proof. Let a be prime to mn and d = gcd(a, m). Then d I a and d Im 
and this implies d I mn. 
Therefore gcd( a, mn) > d, but gcd( a, mn) = 1 by assumption. 
Hence d = 1 proving that a is prime to m. By similar arguments, a 
is prime ton. 
Conversely, let a be prime to m and a be prime to n. Since a is prime to 
m, there exist integers u and v such that au + mv = 1. Since a is prime 
to n, there exist integers p and q such that ap + nq = 1. 
We have anuq + mnvq = nq = 1 - ap 
or, a(nuq + p) + mn(vq) = 1. 
Since nuq + p and vq are integers, it follows that a is prime to mn. 
Lemma 2. If r be the residue of a modulo n and r is prime ton, then 
a is prime to n. 
Since gcd(qn + r, n) = gcd(r, n), the lemma follows. 
Lemma 3. If c be an integer and a is prime ton, then the number of 
integers in the set 
{ c, c + a, c + 2a, ... , c + (n - l)a} that are prime to 
n is </>(n). 
Proof. No two integers of the set are congruent modulo n, because 
c + sa = c + ta ( mod n) 
0 < s < t < n - 1 
=> s = t ( mod n), a contradiction. 
Therefore the set of integers is congruent modulo n to 0, 1, 2, ... , n-1 
in some order. Since the number of integers among 0, 1, 2, ... , n -1 that 
are prime to n is ¢( n), the lemma follows.

3. If n > 2 then cp(n) is an even integer. 
Proof. Case 1. Let n be a power of 2. Let n = 2k, ~ > 1. 
Then </>(n) = </>(2k) = 2k(l _ ½) = 2k-l, an even integer. 
Case 2. Let n be not a power of 2. Then n has an odd prime divisor, 
say, p. Let n = p0 q, where a> 1 and p, q are prime to each other. 
Then cp(n) = cp(p0 ).cp(q), since p0 , q are prime to each other 
= p0 (1- l ).cp(q) = p0 - 1(p - l).cp(q). 
Since (p - 1) is even, it follows that </>( n) is even. 
This completes the proof. 
Another proof of Euclid's theorem on infinitude of primes. 
Let the number of primes be finite and P1, P2, ... , Pm be the enumer-
ation of all primes. Let p = P1P2 ... Pm· 
Let k be an integer satisfying 2 < k < p. Then by the fundamental 
theorem, k is either a prime, i.e., one of P1, P2, ... , Pm, or k has a prime 
divisor, say q which is one of Pl, P2, . .. , Pm. 
Thus in any case, gcd(k,p) > 1. Therefore every integer k satisfying 
1 < k < p is not prime to p and this gives cp(p) = 1. 
But p is clearly an integer > 2 and by the property of the phi function, 
</>( n) is even for every integer n > 2. We arrive at a contradiction. 
Therefore the number of primes is infinite and this completes the proof.

,rbeorem 3.5.4. Fermat's theorem. 
If p be a prime and pis not a divisor of a, then aP-1 -
1 (mod p). 
proof. Let us consider the integers a, 2a, 3a, . . . , (p _ 1 )a. 
None of these is_ divisible by p. Also no two of these are congruent 
Illodulo P, because if ra = sa. (mod p) for some integers r, s satisfying 
1 < r < s < P - 1, then c~ncelhng a ( since a is prime to p) we must have 
r == s ( mod p), a contradiction. 
This means that the integers a, 2a, 3a, .. . , (p - l)a are congruent to 
1, 2, 3, ... , p - 1 modulo p, taken in some order. 
Therefore a.2a.3a .... (p - l)a = 1.2.3 . ... (p - 1) (mod p) 
or, 
aP-1(p - 1)! - (p - 1)! (mod p). 
Since gcd(p, (p - 1)!) = 1, this is equivalent to aP-1 = 1 (mod p). 
This completes the proof. 
Note. This theorem is popularly known as "Fermat's Little Theorem" 
to distinguish it from Fermat's "Great Theorem" or "Last Theorem", 
which states that "if n > 2, then the Diophantine equation xn + yn = zn 
has no solution in integers, other than the trivial solution in which at 
least one of the variables is zero." 
Note. The theorem may be stated in a more general form -
If p be a prime and a is any integer, aP = a ( mod p). 
When p is not a divisor of a, aP-1 = 1 ( mod p) and in this case aP = a 
(mod p) holds. And when pis a divisor of a, aP = 0 (mod p) and it can 
be expressed as aP = a ( mod p).

Theorem 3.5.5. Euler's theorem. 
H n be a positive integer and a is prime ton, then a<l>(n) = 1 (mod n). 
Proof. 
The theorem holds trivially if n = 1. 
Let_ n > 1 and let 
a1 , a2 , ••• , a<t>(n) be positive integers less than n and prime ton. 
Let us consider the integers aa1, aa2, • • • , aa</>(n)· Each of these is 
prime to n, since gcd( a, n) = 1 and gcd( ai, n) = 1 =} gcd( aai, n) == 1. 
No two of the integers aa1,aa2, ... ,aa</>(n) are congruent modulo n 
because aai = aai (mod n) => ai = ai (mod n), since gcd(a, n) = 1, hui 
ai ¢. ai (mod n). 
Therefore the integers aa1, aa2, .. . , aarp(n) contain exactly ¢(n) ele-
ments and they are congruent to the integers a1, a2, . •. , a<l>(n) modulo n 
in some order. 
Multiplying the ¢( n) congruences, we have 
aa1.aa2 . .. aac/>(n) = a1,a2 .. . aq,(n) (mod n). 
Since gcd(ai, n) = 1 for each i, it follows that gcd(a1a2 ... a<t>(n), n) = 1. 
Therefore we can cancel a1a2 ... a</>(n) from both sides of the congruence 
and obtain a<t>(n) - 1 (mod n). 
· This completes the proof. 
Note 1. Euler's theorem is a generalisation of Fermat's theorem. 
Euler's theorem holds for any integer n. If, in particular, n be a 
prime, then ¢( n) = n - 1 and in that case, for any integer a prime to n, 
Euler's theorem gives an-l = 1 (mod n). 
Note 2. If m be a positive integer and gcd( a, m) = 1, the linear congru-
ence ax= 1 (mod m) has a unique solution (mod m). Euler's theorem 
suggests a method of finding such a solution. Since a<l>(m) = 1 (mod m), 
the unique solution is given by x = a<l>(m)-I (mod m). 
Theorem 3.5.6. Wilson's theorem. 
If p be a prime, then (p - 1)! + 1 = 0 (mod p). 
Proof. 
Let a be one of the integers 1, 2, 3, ... ,P - 1. Then no two of 
the integers La, 2.a, 3.a, .. . , (p - 1 )a are congruent modulo p, because if 
ra = sa (mod p) for some integers r, s satisfying 1 < r < s < p - l, 
then cancelling a ( since a is prime to p) we must have r = s( mod P), 6 
contradiction. Also none of these is divisible by p. 
This means that the integers a, 2a, 3a, ... , (p _ l)a are congruent to 
1, 2, 3, • • • ,p-1 modulo P, taken in some order. Hence for every a, there 

INTEGERS 
165 
is one a', and only one, such that a' a = l ( mod p). 
a2 = 1 (mod P) holds if P I ( a2 - 1) and this happens only when 
p I (a - 1) or PI (a+ 1). Since pis a prime and a< p, it follows that in 
this case either a= 1 or a= p - 1. 
Therefore 1 and p-1 are the only values of a for which a.a= 1 (mod 
p). 
If we omit integers 1 and p - 1, the remaining integers 2, 3, ... ,P - 2 
are such that they are grouped into pairs a, a' for which a.a' = 1 (mod 
p) holds. Multiplying ½(P - 3) pairs of such congruences, we have 
2.3 ... (p - 2) = 1 (mod p) 
or, (p - 2)! = 1 (mod p). 
Multiplying by p- 1, (p-1)! = p - l (mod p)= -1 (mod p) which is 
equivalent to (p- 1)! + 1 = 0 {mod p). 
This completes the proof. 
Let us take an example to elucidate the proof of the theorem. 
Let p = 11. Then the integers 1, 2, 3, ... , 10 are grouped into pairs 
a, a' such that a.a' = 1 (mod 11). 
1.1 = 1 (mod 11), 2.6 = 1 (mod 11), 3.4 = 1 {mod 11), 
5.9 = 1 (mod 11), 7.8 = 1 (mod 11), 10.10 = 1 (mod 11). 
Omitting the first and the last and multiplying the remaining, 
9! = 1 (mod 11) 
or, 10! = 10 (mod 11)= -1 (mod 11) 
or, 
10! + 1 = 0 (mod 11). 
Note 1. The converse of Wilson's theorem is also true. 
If (p - 1)! + 1 = O (mod p) then p must be prime. For, if p be not 
a prime, then p has a divisor d with 1 < d < p such that d divides 
(p - 1) ! + 1. Again since 1 < d < p, d divides one of the factors in 
(p- 1)!. Therefore d divides both (p- 1)! + 1 and (p - 1)! which yields 
d I 1, an absurdity. 
Note 2. Wilson's theorem and its converse, taken together, provide a 
necessary and sufficient condition for determining primality of a positive 
integer n. But this test is impracticable since ( n - 1) ! becomes very large 
as n assumes large values. 
We now come to a theorem of Dirichlet that establishes the existence 
of infinity of primes in the arithmetic progression 1 + 4n, n E N. 

166 
HIGHER ALGEBRA 
Theorem 3.5. 7. (Dirichlet) There are infinitely many primes of the 
form 4n+ 1. 
Proof. Let N be any positive integer. We shall show that there exists a 
prime > N of the form 4n + 1. 
Let m = (N!)2 + 1. Then mis odd and m > 1. None of the numbers 
2, 3, ... , N is a divisor of m. Let p be the smallest prime divisor of rn. 
Thenp > N. 
We have (N!)2 = -1 (mod p). 
Therefore (N!)P-1 = (-1)9 (mod p). 
But (N!)P-1 = 1 (mod p), by Fermat's theorem. 
Hence (-l)E? = 1 (mod p). 
(-1) p;i is either 1 or -1. If (-1)9 = -1, then -1 = 1 (mod p) 
must hold. But it is an impossibility, since p is odd. Therefore ( -1) ~ 
is 1 and therefore P; 1 is even. That is, p = I ( mod 4). 
Thus for every positive integer N there exists a prime p > N such 
that p = I ( mod 4) and consequently, the number of primes of the form 
4n + 1 is infinite. 
This completes the proof.

3.6. Greatest integer function. 
For any real number x there exists an integer n such that n < 
' 
1· 
b t 
- :t" 
n + l, i.e., either x is an integer n or x ies e ween two consecutive 
integers n and n + 1. Therefore x can be expressed as 
x = n+ f, where n is an integer and O < f < 1. 
The integer n is said to be the integral part of x and is denoted b 
[x]. f is said to be the fractional part of x and f = x - [x]. 
y 
So we have the relation [x] < x < [x] + 1 for any real number x. As 
every real number x corresponds to an integer [x] which is the greatest 
integer not greater than x by the correspondence [ ] , [ ] is said to be the 
greatest integer function. 
For example, [.3] = 0, [3] = 3, [-.3) = -1, [1r] = 3, [v'2] = 1.

Another proof of Fermat's theorem. 
If p be a prime and pis not a divisor of n, nP-1 = 1 (mod p). 
Proof. First we prove that if p be a prime, nP = n ( mod p) for all positive 
integers n, by using the principle of induction. 
The statement nP = n ( mod p) is trivially true for n = 1. 
Let us assume that the statement is true for n = k, k > 1. 
Then kP = k (mod p). 
Now (k + l)P = kP +v c1kp- l +P c2kv-2 + · · · +P ep_1k + 1 
= kP + M(p) + 1, where M(p) =P c1kP-l +P c2kP-2 + · • • +v Cp-1k. 
M(p) is divisible by p, since Per is divisible by p for O < r < p. 
Therefore ( k + 1 )P = k + 1 ( mod p) and this shows that the statement 
nP = n (mod p) is true for n = k + 1, if it be true for n = k. By 
the principle of induction, the statement nP = n (mod p) is true for all 
natural numbers n. 
Case 1. n is a positive integer prime top. 
Cancelling n from both sides of the congruence nP _ n (mod p), we 
have nP-l = 1 (mod p). 
Case 2. n is a negative integer prime top. 
There exist integers q and r such that n = pq + r , where O < r < p. 
Then nP-1 = rP-1 (mod p), where r is a positive integer < p 
= 1 (mod p), by case 1. 
This completes the proof.

3. 7. Mobius function. 
Definition. The Mobius function µ is defined for all positive integers 
by 
µ(1) = 1; 
µ(n) = {-l)k if n = P1P2 .. ·Pk, where P1,P2, ... ,Pk are distinct 
prrmes; 
µ( n) = 0 if p2 In for some prime p. 
To be explicit, µ(n) = 0 if n is not square-free and µ(n) = (-l)k if n 
is square free and is the product of k distinct primes. 
For example, µ(1) = 1, µ(2) = -1, µ{3) = - 1, µ(4) = 0, µ(5) = -1, 
µ(6) = 1, µ(7) = -1, µ(8) = 0, µ(9) = 0, µ(10) = 1. 
Theorem 3. 7.1. The functionµ is a multiplicative function. That is, if 
m and n be relatively prime positive integers, then µ(mn) = µ(m)µ(n). 
Proof Case 1. Either m = 1 or n = 1. 
If m = 1, µ(mn) = µ(n) = µ(m)µ(n), since µ(m) = 1. 
If n = 1, µ(mn) = µ(m) = µ(m)µ(n), since µ(n) = 1. 
Case 2. Either m or n has a prime square divisor.

3.8. Diophantine equation x 2 + y2 = z
2 
• 
A triplet of positive integers x, y, z satisfying the equa~ion x
2 + y2 ::: 
z 2 is called a Pythagorean triplet. x, y, z represent the si?es of a right 
triangle, z being the hypotenuse and x, y the legs of the tnangle. 
If x, y, z be a Pythagorean triplet, then kx, ky, kz is also a 
Pythagorean triplet for all integers k > 1 and they represent the sides of 
a similar triangle. 
A Pythagorean triplet x, y, z is called a primitive Pythagorean triplet 
if gcd(x, y, z) = 1. A primitive triplet corresponds to the smallest triangle 
with integer sides among all similar triangles. 
For example, 3, 4, 5; 6, 8, 10; 9, 12, 15; ... are all Pythagorean triplets 
representing similar right triangles, but 3, 4, 5 is the primitive triplet 
corresponding to this class of similar triangles. 
Some properties of primitive Pythagorean triplets. 
1. If x, y, z be a primitive triplet, then x, y, z are pairwise prime to each 
other. 
Proof gcd(x, y, z) = 1. Let gcd(x, y) = d > 1. Then dlx, dly. Let p be 
a prime divisor of d. Then plx,ply, This implies plx2 ,ply2 and therefore 
plz2 and this again implies plz, This contradicts that gcd(x, y, z) = 1. 
Therefore gcd(x, y) = 1. 
Similarly, gcd(y, z) = 1, gcd(x, z) = 1. 
2. If x, y, z be a primitive triplet, then z is odd and x , y are of different 
parity (i.e., one of them is odd and the other is even.) 
Proof Since gcd(x, y) = 1, x, y cannot be both even. 
Let x, y be both odd. Then x is either of the forms 4n + 1, 4n + 3. 
In any case, x2 is of the form 4n + 1. Similarly, y2 is of the form 4n + 1. 
So z 2 is of the form 4n + 2. Since for any integer, odd or even, a perfect 
square is either of the form 4n or of the form 4n + 1, z2 cannot be of the 
form 4n + 2. Therefore x, y cannot be both odd. 
Hence one of x, Y is odd and the other is even and therefore z is odd, 
To be definite, we shall take x odd, y even and of course z odd in a 
primitive Pythagorean triplet x, y, z.

For every positive integer r, there is a Pythagorean triangle with in-
radius r. Bec~us~ we may take m-n = 1 and n = r, i.e., m = r+l, n = r. 
The triangle 1s given by x = 2r + 1, y = 2r(r + 1), z = 2r2 + 2r + l. 
There may be other triangles also. If there be an odd prime divisor p 
of r, then we may take m - n = P and n = ~ giving a different triangle. 
Worked Examples. 
1. If x, y, z be a primitive Pythagorean triplet, prove that 12jxy. 
x = m
2 
- n2, Y = 2mn, z = m2 +n2 , where m, n are positive integers 
prime to each other with m > n and m, n are of different parity. 
Since one of m, n is even, y is a multiple of 4 and therefore 4jxy. 
If one of m, n is a multiple of 3, then y is a multiple of 3 and therefore 
3lxy. 
If none of m, n is a multiple of 3 then by Fermat's theorem, m 2 = 1 
(mod 3) and n2 = 1 (mod 3). Therefore m 2 - n2 = 0 (mod 3), i.e., 3lx 
and therefore 3 I xy. 
4lxy and 3jxy => 12lxy, since gcd(3, 4) = 1. 
2. If x, y, z be a primitive Pythagorean triplet, prove that 60jxyz. 
x = m 2 -n2 , y = 2mn, z = m2 +n2 , where m, n are positive integers 
prime to each other with m > n and m, n are of different parity.

5lxyz.

3. If r be an odd prime, show that there are two Pythagorean triangles 
with in-radius r. 
The in-radius r of a Pythagorean triangle whose sides are x = m2 -
n2, y = 2mn, z = m 2 + n2, where m, n are positive integers of opposite 
Parity with m > n and gcd( m, n) = 1 is given by r = n( m - n), where 
m - n is odd and n is prime to m - n. 
There are two choices. We may take (i) m - n = I and n = r giving 
a triangle, (ii) m _ n =rand n = 1 giving a different triangle. 
Thus there are two Pythagorean triangles with in-radius r.

4.1. Polynomials. 
An expression of the form 
n +

rational integral function of x. 
ao,a1,•••,an are called coefficients and a0xn,a1xx-1, ••• ,an are 
called terms of the polynomial. If ao i= 0, the polynomial is said to 
be of degree n and the term aoxn is called the leading term. 
The general form of a polynomial of degree 1 is a0x + a1, a0 i= O; and 
of degree 2 is aox2 + a1x + a2, ao i= 0. 
A non-zero constant a0 itself is said to be a polynomial of degree 0 
while a polynomial all of whose coefficients are zero is said to be a zero 
polynomial and is denoted by 0 and no degree is assigned to it. 
Since a polynonial is an expression containing the variable x, it is 
denoted by f(x),g(x) etc. The value of the polynomial f(x) for x = a 
where a is a real number or a complex number is denoted by / (a). 
In particular, if the coefficients ao, a1, a2, ... of a polynomial f(x) be 
all real ·numbers, the polynomial / ( x) is said to be a real polynomial.

Theorem 4.1.3. Factor theorem. 
If J ( x) be a polynomial then x - a: is a factor of f ( x) if and only if 
/(o.) = 0.

4.2. Synthetic division. 
We have seen that when a polynomial / ( x) is divided by x - a the 
remainder can be obtained readily without going into laborious process 
of division algorithm. 
We now develop a simple method of obtaining the quotient when f (x)

5. THEORY OF EQUATIONS 
5.1. Algebraic equations. 
Let f(x) be a polynomial in x of degree > 1 whose co-efficients are 
real or complex numbers. 
Then / ( x) = 0 is said to be an algebraic equation or a polynomial 
equation. The degree of the polynomial f ( x) is said to be the degree of 
the equation f ( x) = 0. 
An equation of degree 1 is a0x + a1 = 0, where ao =/- 0. 
An equation of degree 2 is a0x2 + a1x + a2 = 0, where ao =/- 0. This 
is said to be a quadratic equation. 
An equation of degree 3 is a0x 3 + a 1 x 2 + a2x + a3 = 0, where ao =/- 0. 
This is said to be a cubic equation. 
An equation of degree 4 is a0x4 + a1x 3 + a2x2 + aax + a 4 = 0, where 
ao-::/= 0. This is said to be a biquadratic equation. 
The general "form of an algebraic equation of degree n is a0xn + 
a1xn-l + · • • +an-ix+ an = 0, where ao, a1, ... , an are real or com-
plex numbers and a0 =/- 0. 
If a be a value of x for which f(x) becomes zero, i.e., if /(o.) = 0, 
then o: is said to be a root of the equation f ( x) = 0. 
The existence of such an o. for which f(x) becomes zero is assured by 
a theorem, called the Fundamental theorem of classical algebra, 
which states that 
every algebraic equation has a root, real or complex. 
Here we take the theorem without proof and derive some important 
deductions from it. 
Theorem 5.1.1. An algebraic equation of degree n has n roots and no 
more. 
Proof. Let f (x) = a0xn + a 1xn-l + · · · + an be a polynomial with co-
efli · 
cients real or complex, of degree n. 'Then ao =I= 0. 
The equation f ( x) = O is an algebraic equation of degree n. 
By the fundamental theorem, this equation has a root, say o 1.

Definition. If / ( x) be a zero polynomial then / ( x) = 0 is said to be an 
identity. In other words, if /(x) and g(x) be two identical polynomials 
then J(x) = g(x) is said to be an identity. 
Examples.

3 zero polynomial.

ax + 1) + c( x2 + bx + 1) is an identity, because the equality is satisfied 
by x = a, x = b, x = c. 
We have seen that if / ( x) = 0 is a polynomial equation of degree n 
then the equation has n roots, say 01, 02, ... , an. In this case f(x) can 
be expressed as the product of n linear factors ao(x-a1)(x-a2) ... (x-
an), ao being the leading co-efficient of the polynomial f(x). The factors 
need not be all distinct. If may be that a1 = 02 = . . . = O'.r. In this 
case (x - a 1)r is a factor of f(x) and a 1 is said to be a multiple root of 
order r. There may be many multiple roots of /(x) = 0. If however, each 
root is counted according to its multiplicity, the number of roots of the 
equation f(x) = 0 is exactly n. 
If a be a root of the polynomial equation / ( x) = 0 of multiplicity r, 
then (x - at is a factor of f(x). 
Then /(x) = (x-at</>(x), where <f,(x) is a polynomial of degree n-r 
and </>(a) -I 0, because </>(a)= 0 would imply x-a is a factor of </>(x) and 
as such (x-a)r+l would be a factor of f(x) contradicting the multiplicity 
of a.

Theorem 5.2.1. Han equation with real coefficients has a complex root 
a + i/3 then it has also the conjugate complex root a _ i/3. 
In other words, in an equation with real coefficients imaginary roots 
occur in conjugate pairs. 
proof. Let f ( x) = 0 be an equation of degree n with real coefficients and 
let a + i/3 be a root of / ( x) = 0. It is obvious that n > 2. 
Let us divide f(x) by the product {x - (a+ if3)}{x - (a - i/3)}, i.e., 
by (x - a)2 + /32 - Let q(x) be the quotient and r(x) be the remainder. 
Then the degree of q(x) is n - 2 and the degree of r(x) is at most one. 
Since f(x) is a real polynomial and (x - a)2 + {32 is also a real 
quadratic, q(x) and r(x) are both real polynomials and we assume 
r(x) =ax+ b where a and bare both real. 
Therefore f(x) = [(x - a)2 + /32]q(x) +ax+ b. 
Since a+ i/3 is a root, f(a + i/3) = 0, i.e., a(a + i/3) + b = 0 
or, ( an + beta) + ia/3 = 0 and this implies aa + b = 0, a/3 = 0. 
But /3 I 0. Therefore a= 0 and consequently, b = 0. 
So f(x) = [(x - a)2 + f32]q(x). 
J(o: - i/3) = [(a - i/3 - a)2 + f32]q(a - i/3) = 0 and this proves that 
a - i/3 is a root of the equation f(x) = 0. 
This completes the proof. 
Note. If a+i/3 be a multiple complex root of f(x) = 0 whose coefficients 
are all real, of multiplicity r then a - i/3 is also a multiple root of the 
equation f(x) = 0 of multiplicity r. 
From the theorem it follows that imaginary roots of a polynomial 
I 
equation with real coefficients occur always in pairs. Therefore in such 
an equation the number of imaginary roots is always even. 
If the degree of a polynomial equation with real coefficients be odd 
then it follows that the equation has at least a real root. If however, the 
degree be even then the equation may not have a real root at all. 
If f ( x) be a polynomial with real coefficients, to each linear factor x -
<l-i{3 of f(x) corresponding to an imaginary root a+i/3 there corresponds 
another linear factor x - a+i/3 corresponding to the conjugate root a-i/3 
and the product (x-a-if3)(x-a+if3) = (x-a)2+,82 is a real quadratic 
factor of J(x). 
Therefore we can say that a real polynomial can always be expressed 
as the Product of real linear and real quadratic factors.

5_3.1. Limits of real roots. 
211 
Let a1, 0 2, · · ·, 0 r b~ the ~eal roots of an equation f(x) = O. The 
number of real roots being finite, there exist two real numbers u and l 
such that l < ai < u for i = 1, 2, ... , r. 
u is said to be an upper limit and l is said to be a lower limit of the 
real roots. 
Let the equation f ( x) = 0 be written with the coefficient of the leading 
term positive. 
A real number u is an upper limit of the real roots of the equation 
J(x) = 0 if f(x) > 0 for all x > u. 
A real number l is a lower limit of the real roots of the equation 
J(x) = 0 if it is an upper limit of the equation f(-x) = O. 
A real number h is said to be a lower limit of the positive roots of 
f(x) = 0, if¼ is an upper limit of the positive roots of/(¼) = 0.

5.3.4. Descartes' rule of signs. 
· In a sequence of real numbers ao, a1, ... , an, none of which is zero, 
the signs of two consecutive elements may be same or different. When 
same sign occurs we say that the elements show a continuation of signs; 
when the signs are different we say that the elements show a variation of 
signs. 
For example, in the sequence 1, 3, -5, -7, 9, -4, 10, 2 there are 3 con-
tinuations and 4 variations of signs. 
If some of the elements of a sequence be zero, we ignore their presence 
in the sequence and count the number of continuations and variations of 
signs. 
For example, in the sequence 1, 3, -2, 0, -3, 0, 4, 0, 0, 7 there are 3 
continuations and 2 variations of signs. 
We state here without proof a celebrated theorem of Descartes, 
known as "Descartes' rule of signs". 
Statement of the rule. 
The number of positive roots of an equation f ( x) = O with real coeffi· 
cients does not exceed the number of variations of signs in the sequence 
of the coefficients of f(x) and if less, it is less by an even number. 
This says that if v be the number of variations of signs and r be the 
number of positive roots, then v = r + 2h where h is a non-negative 
integer. 
Deductions. 
1. The number of negative roots of an equation J ( x) = O with real coef-
ficients does not exceed the number of variations of signs in the sequence 
of coefficients of f(-x) and if less, it is less by an even number. 
Proof. Let r' be the number of negative roots of the equation f(x)::::: o. 

THEORY OF EQUATIONS 
215 
Then r' is the number ?f ?ositive _root~ the equation f(-x) = o. Let 
, be the number of variations of signs 1n the sequence of coefficients of 
f (-x). By the rule, v' = r' + 2h where his a non-negative integer. 
2 If J(x) = 0 be an equation of degree n with real coefficients having 
~ zero root and v, v' are respectively the number of variations of signs 
: the sequence of coefficients of f(x) and f(-x) such that v + v' < n, 
then the equation f(x) = 0 has at least n - (v + v') complex roots. 
proof. Let r be the number of positive roots and r' be that of negative 
roots of the equation f(x) = 0. Then by the rule, v = r + 2h and 
v' = r' + 2h' where h, h' are non-negative integers. 
Therefore r < v, r' < v' and n - (r + r') > n - (v + v'). 
But n - (r + r') is the number of complex roots of the equation 
f (x) = 0 and the assertion is established. 
3. If all the roots of the equation f(x) = 0 be non-zero real and v~ v' are 
respectively the number of variations of signs in the sequence of coeffi-
cients of f ( x) and / ( - x) then the equation f ( x) = 0 has v positive roots 
and v' negative roots. 
Proof. Let r, r' be respectively the number of positive and negative roots 
of f(x) = 0 and let n be its degree. Then r+r' = n. Again the minimum 
number of complex roots of the equation is n - ( v + v'), by deduction 2. 
Since all the roots are real, n - (v + v') = 0. Therefore r + r' = 
v+v' = n. 
By tbe rule, v > rand v' > .r'~ But v > rand v+v' = r+r' =} v' < r', 
a contradiction. 
Therefore v = r and consequently v' = r'.
